Precedent	Article 30(2) of the Constitution reinforces this when it says, "The <ORG> shall not, in granting aid to educational inst itutions, discriminate against any educational institution on the ground that it is under the management of a minority, whether based on religion or language".
Precedent	This assumes that even after the grant of aid by the <ORG> to an educational institution under the management of the minority, the educational institution continues to be a minority educational institution.
Precedent	According to some, Article 30(2) merely protects the minority's right of management of the educational institution and not the students who form part of such institution.
Precedent	Such a reading would be contrary to Article 30(1) itself.
Precedent	The argument is based on the construction of the word 'management'.
Precedent	'Management' may be defined as 'the process of managing' and is not limited to the people managing the institution.Concise Oxford Dictionary (10th Edition) 864.
Precedent	In the context of Article 30(1) and having regard to the content of the right, namely, the education of the minority community, the word 'management' in Article 30(2) must be construed to mean the 'p rocess and not the 'persons' in management 'Aid' by definition means to give support or to held or assist.
Precedent	It cannot be that by giving 'aid' one destroys those to whom 'aid' is given.
Precedent	The obvious purpose of Article 30(2) is to forbid the <ORG> from refusing aid to a minority educational institution merely because it is being run as a minority educational institution.
Precedent	Besides Article 30(2) is an additional right conferred on minorities under Article 30(1).
Precedent	It cannot be construed in a manner which is destructive of or as a limitation on Article 30(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	As has been said earlier by this <ORG> in Rev.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>, supra Clause (2) of Article 30 is only another non-discriminatory clause in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a right in addition to the rights under Article 30(1) and does not operate to derogate from the provisions in Clause (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	When in decision after decision, this <ORG> has held that aid in whatever form is necessary for an educational institution to survive, it is a specious argument to say that a minori ty institution can preserve its rights under Article 30(1) by refusing aid.
Precedent	305.
Precedent	I would, therefore, respectfully agree with the conclusion expressed in the majority opinion that grant of aid under Article 30(2) cannot be used as a lever to take away the rights of the minorities under Article 30(1).
Precedent	306.
Precedent	Articles 29(2) and 30(1) Article 29(2) says that "No citizen shall be denied admission into any educational institution maintained by the <ORG> or receiving aid out of <ORG> funds on grounds only of religion, race, case, language or any of them".
Precedent	307.
Precedent	It is because Article 30(1) covers the right to admit students that there is an apparent conflict between Article 29(2) and Article 30(1).
Precedent	There are two ways of considering the relationship between Article 30(1) and Article 29(2 ), the first in the context of Article 14 , the second by an interpretation of Article 29(2) itself.
Precedent	308.
Precedent	Article 29(2) has not been expressed as a positive right.
Precedent	Nevertheless in substance it confers a right on a person not to be d enied admission into an aided institution only on the basis of religion, race etc.
Precedent	The language of Article 29(2) reflects the language used in other non-discriminatory Articles in the Constitution namely, Clauses (1) and (2) of Article 15 and Clauses (1) and (2) of Article 16.
Precedent	As already noted both the Articles contain exceptions which permit laws being made which make special provisions on the basis of sex, caste and race.
Precedent	Even in the absence of Clauses (3) and (4) of Article 15 and Clauses (3), (4) and 4(A) of Article 16 , <ORG> could have made special provisions on the forbidden bases of race, caste or sex, provided that the basis was not the only reason for creating a separate class.
Precedent	There would have to be an additional rational factor qualifying such basis to bring it within the concept of 'equality in fact' on the principle of 'rational classification'.
Precedent	For example when by law a reservation is made in favour of a member of a backward class in the matter of appointment, the reservation is no doubt m ade on the basis of caste.
Precedent	It is also true that to the extent of the reservation other citizens are discriminated against on one of the bases prohibited under Article 16(1).
Precedent	Nevertheless such legislation would be valid because the reservation is not only on the basis of caste/race but because of the additional factor of their backwardness.
Precedent	Clauses (3) and (4) of Article 15 like Clause 3, 4 and 4(A) of Article 16 merely make explicit what is otherwise implicit in the concept of equality under Article 14.
Precedent	309.
Precedent	By the same token, Article 29(2) does not create an absolute right for citizens to be admitted into any educational institution maintained by the <ORG> or receiving aid out of <ORG> funds.
Precedent	It does not prohibit the denial of admission on grounds other than religion, race, caste or language.
Precedent	Therefore, reservation of admissions on the grounds of residence, occupation of parents or other bases has been held to be a valid classification which does not derogate from the principles of equality under Article 1 4.
Precedent	[See: <CITATION_195765> : <CITATION_1276295> : 1971 SCR (Supp.)
Precedent	608.
Precedent	Even in respect of the "prohibited" bases, like the other non-discriminatory Articles, Article 29(2) is constitutionally subject to the principle of 'rational classification'.
Precedent	If a person is denied admission on the basis of a constitutional right, that is not a denial only on the basis of religion, race etc.
Precedent	This is exemplified in Article 15(4) which provides for: "Nothing in this article or in Clause (2) of Article 29 shall prevent the <ORG> from making any special provision for the advancement of any socially and educationally backward classes of citizens or for the Scheduled Castes and the Tribes."
Precedent	310.
Precedent	To the extent that legislation is enacted under Article 15(4) making special provision in respect of a particular caste, there is a denial of admission to others who do not belong to the caste.
Precedent	Nevertheless, Article 15(4) does not contradict the right under Article 29(2).
Precedent	This is because of the use of the word 'only' in Article 29(2).
Precedent	Article 15(4) is based on the rationale that Schedule Castes and Tribes are not on par with other members of society in the matter of education and, therefore, special provision is to be made for them.
Precedent	It is not, therefore, only caste but this additional factor which prevents clause 15(4) from conflicting with Article 29(2) and Article 14.
Precedent	311.
Precedent	Then again, under Article 337 , grants are made available for the benefit of the Anglo-Indian community in respect of education, provided that any educational institution receiving such grant makes available at least 40% of the annual admission for members of communities other than the Anglo-Indian community.
Precedent	Hence 60% of the admission to an aided <ORG> is constitutionally reservable for members of the Anglo-Indian community.
Precedent	To the extent of such reservation, there is necessarily a denial of admission to non-Anglo Indians on the basis of race.
Precedent	312.
Precedent	Similarly, the Constitution has also carved out a further exception to Article 29(2) in the form of Art icle 30(1) by recognising the rights of special classes in the form of minorities based on language or religion to establish and administer educational institutions of their choice.
Precedent	The right of the minorities under Article 30(1) does not operate as discrimination against other citizens only on the ground of religion or language.
Precedent	The reason for such classification is not only religion or language per se but minorities based on religion and language.
Precedent	Although, it is not necessary to justify a classification made by the Constitution, this fact of 'minorityship' is the obvious rationale for making a distinction, the underlying assumption being that minorities by their very numbers are in a politically disadvantaged situation and require special protection at least in the field of education.
Precedent	313.
Precedent	Articles 15(4), 337 and 30 are therefore facets of substantive equality by making special provision for special classes on special considerations.
Precedent	314.
Precedent	Even on general principles of interpretation, it cannot be held th at Article 29(2) is absolute and in effect wipes out Article 30(1).
Precedent	Article 29(2) refers to ' any educational institution' -- the word "any" signifying the generality of its application.
Precedent	Article 30(1) on the other hand refers to 'educational institutions established and administered by minorities'.
Precedent	Clearly, the right under Article 30(1) is the more particular right and on the principle of ' generalia speciaiibus non derogent, it must be held that Article 29(2) does not override he educational institutions even if they are aided under Article 30(1)  <CITATION_944601> : 1959 Suppl.
Precedent	1 SCR 806, 860, 1939 FCR 18.
Precedent	315.
Precedent	Then again Article 29(2) appears under the heading 'Protection of interests of minorities'.
Precedent	Whatever the historical reasons for the placement of Article 29(2) under this head, it is clear that no general principles of interpretation, the heading is at least a pointer or aid in construing the meaning of Article 29(2).
Precedent	As <NAME>, J said "if there is any doubt in the interpretation of the words in  the section, the heading certainly helps us to resolve that doubt."
Precedent	<CITATION_535729> .
Precedent	Therefore, if two interpretations of the words of Article 29(2) are possible, the one which is in keeping with the heading of the Article must be preferred.
Precedent	It would follow that Article 29(2) must be construed in a manner protective of minority interests and not destructive of them.
Precedent	316.
Precedent	When 'aid' is sought for by the minority institution to run its institution for the benefit of students belonging to that particular community, the argument on the basis of Article 29(2) is that if such an institution asks for aid it does so at the peril of depriving the very persons for whom aid was asked for in the first place.
Precedent	Apart from this anomalous result, if the taking of aid implies that the minority institution will be forced to give up or waive its right under Article 30(1 ), then on the principle that it is not permissible to give up or waive fundamental rights, such an interpretation is not possible.
Precedent	It has then been urged th at Article 29(2) applies to minority institutions under Article 30(1) much in the same way that Article 28(1) and 28(3) do.
Precedent	The argument proceeds on the assumption that an educational institution set up under Article 30(1) is set up for the purposes and with the sole object of giving religious instruction.
Precedent	The assumption is wrong.
Precedent	At the outset, it may also be noted that Article 28(1) and (3) do not in terms apply to linguistic minority educational institutions at all.
Precedent	Furthermore, the right to set up an educational institution in which religious instruction is to be imparted is a right which is derived from Article 26(a) which provides that every religious denomination or any section thereof shall have the right to establish and maintain institutions for religious and charitable purposes, and not under Article 30(1).
Precedent	Educational institutions set up under Article 26(a) are, therefore, subject to Clauses (1) and (3) of Article 28.
Precedent	Article 30(1) is a right additional to Article 26(a).
Precedent	This follows from the fac t that it has been separately and expressly provided for and there is nothing in the language of Article 30(1) making the right thereunder subject to Articles 25 and 26.
Precedent	Unless it is so construed Article 30(1) would be rendered redundant <ORG>, paras 7 to 12.
Precedent	Therefore, what Article 30 does is to secure the minorities the additional right to give general education.
Precedent	Although in a particular case a minority educational institution may combine general education with religious instruction that is done in exercise of the rights derivable from Article 26(a) and Article 30(1) and not under Article 30(1) alone.
Precedent	Clauses (1) and (3) of Article 28 , therefore, do not apply to Article 30(1).
Precedent	The argument in support of reading Article 30(1) as being subject to Article 29(2) on the analogy of Article 28(1) and 28(3) is, I would think, erroneous.
Precedent	317.
Precedent	For the reasons already stated I have held the right to admit minority students to a minority educational institutions is an intrinsic part of Article 30(1).
Precedent	To  say that Article 29(2) prevails over Article 30(1) would be to infringe and to a large extent wipe out this right.
Precedent	There would be no distinction between a minority educational institution and other institutions and the rights under Article 30(1) would be rendered wholly inoperational.
Precedent	It is no answer to say that the rights of unaided minority institutions would remain untouched because Article 29(2) does not relate to unaided institutions at all.
Precedent	Whereas if one reads Article 29(2) as subject to Article 30(1) then effect can be given to both.
Precedent	And it is the latter approach which is to be followed in the interpretation of constitutional provisions.
Precedent	<NAME> v. State of Mysore, 1958 SCR 895, 918.
Precedent	In other words, as long as the minority educational institution is being run for the benefit of and catering to the needs of the members of that community under Article 30(1 ), Article 29(2) would not apply.
Precedent	But once the minority educational institution travels beyond the needs in the sense of requireme nts of its own community, at that stage it is no longer exercising rights of admission guaranteed under Article 30(1).
Precedent	To put it differently, when the right of admission is exercised not to meet the need of the minorities, the rights of admission given under Article 30(1) is to that extent removed and the institution is bound to admit students for the balance in keeping with the provisions of Article 29(2).
Precedent	318.
Precedent	A simple illustration would make the position clear.
Precedent	'Aid' is given to a minority institution.
Precedent	There are 100 seats available in that institution.
Precedent	There are 150 eligible candidates according to the procedure evolved by the institution.
Precedent	Of the 150, 60 candidates belong to that particular community and 90 to other communities.
Precedent	The institution will be entitled, under Article 30(1) to admit all 60 minority students first and then fill the balance 40 seats from the other communities without discrimination in keeping with Article 29(2).
Precedent	319.
Precedent	I would, therefore, not subscribe to the view that Article 29(2 ) operates to deprive aided minority institutions the right to admit members of their community to educational institutions established and administered by them either on any principle of interpretation or on any concept of equality or secularism.
Precedent	320.
Precedent	The next task is to consider whether this interpretation of Article 29(2) and 30(1) is discordant with the historical context in which these Articles came to be included in the Constitution.
Precedent	Before referring to the historical context, it is necessary to keep in mind that what is being interpreted are constitutional provisions which "have a content and a significance that vary from age to age".<NAME>: Nature of Judicial Process, p.17.
Precedent	Of particular significance is the content of the concept of equality which has been developed by a process of judicial interpretation over the years as discussed earlier.
Precedent	It is also necessary to be kept in mind that reports of the various Committees appointed by <ORG> and speeches made in <ORG> ly and the record of other proceedings of <ORG> are admissible, if at all, merely as extrinsic aids to construction and do not as such bind the <ORG>.
Precedent	Ultimately it is for this <ORG> to say what is meant by the words of the Constitution.
Precedent	321.
Precedent	The proponents of the argument that Article 29(2) over-rides Article 30(1) have referred to excerpts from the speeches made by members of <ORG> which have been quoted in support of their view.
Precedent	Apart from the doubtfulness as to the admissibility of the speeches, <CITATION_399708> : <CITATION_1195357> , in my opinion, there is nothing in the speeches which shows an intention on the part of <ORG> to abridge in any way the special protection afforded to minorities under Article 30(1).
Precedent	The intention indicated in the speeches relating to the framing of Article 29(2) appears to be an extension of the right of non-discrimination to members of the non-minority in respect of <ORG> aided or <ORG> maintained educational instituti ons.
Precedent	It is difficult to find in the speeches any unambiguous statement which points to a determination on the part of <ORG> to curtail the special rights of the minorities under Article 30(1).
Precedent	Indeed if one scrutinises the broad historical context and the sequence of events preceding the drafting of the Constitution it is clear that one of the primary objectives of the Constitution was to preserve, protect and guarantee the rights of the minorities unchanged by any rule or regulation that may be enacted by <ORG> or any State legislature.
Precedent	322.
Precedent	The history which preluded the independence of this country and the framing of the Constitution highlights the political context in which the Constitution was framed and the political content of the "special" rights given to minorities.
Precedent	I do not intend to burden this judgment with a detailed reference to the historical run-up to the Constitution as ultimately adopted by <ORG> vis-a-vis the rights of the minorities and the impor tance that was placed on enacting effective and adequate constitutional provisions to safeguard their interests.
Precedent	This has been adequately done by <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	in <NAME> v. State of Kerala, on the basis of which the learned Judge came to the conclusion that the rights of the minorities under the Constitution formed part of the basic structure of the Constitution and were un-amendable and inalienable.
Precedent	323.
Precedent	I need only add that the rights of linguistic minorities assumed special significance and support when, much after independence, the imposition of a 'unifying language' led not to unity but to an assertion of differences.
Precedent	States were formed on linguistic bases showing the apparent paradox that allowing for and protecting differences leads to unity and integrity and enforced assimilation may lead to disaffection and unrest.
Precedent	The recognition of the principle of "unity in diversity" has continued to be the hall mark of the Constitution -- a concept which has been further strengthened by affording fur ther support to the protection of minorities on linguistic bases in 1956 by way of Articles 350A and 350B and in 1978 by introducing Clause (1-A) in Article 30 requiring "the <ORG>, that is to say, <ORG> in the case of a <ORG> legislation or a <ORG> legislature in the case of <ORG> legislation, in making a specific law to provide for the compulsory acquisition of the property of minority educational institutions, to ensure that the amount payable to the educational institution for the acquisition of its property will not be such as will in any manner impair the functioning of the educational institution".
Precedent	<ORG> <CITATION_1315823> .
Precedent	Any judicial interpretation of the provisions of the Constitution whereby this constitutional diversity is diminished would be contrary to this avowed intent and the political considerations which underlie this intention.
Precedent	324.
Precedent	The earlier decisions of this <ORG> show that the issue of admission to a minority educational institution almost invariably arose  in the context of the <ORG> claiming that a minority institution had to be 'purely' one which was established and administered by members of the minority community concerned, strictly for the members of the minority community, with the object only of preserving of the minority religion, language, script or culture.
Precedent	The contention on the part of the executive then was that a minority institution could not avail of the protection of Article 30(1) if there was any non-minority element either in the establishment, administration, admission or subjects taught.
Precedent	It was in that context that the Court in Kerala Education Bill held that a 'sprinkling of outsiders' being admitted into a minority institution did not result in the minority institution shedding its character and ceasing to be a minority institution.p.1052.
Precedent	It was also in that context that the Court in <ORG> (supra) came to the conclusion that a minority institution based on religion and language had the right to establish and administer educa tional institution for imparting general secular education and still not lose its minority character.
Precedent	While the effort of the <ORG> was to retain the 'purity' of a minority institution and thereby to limit it.
Precedent	"the principle which can be discerned in the various decisions of this <ORG> is that the catholic approach which led to the drafting of the provisions relating to minority rights should not be set at naught by narrow judicial interpretation".
Precedent	325.
Precedent	The 'liberal, generous and sympathetic approach' of this <ORG> towards the rights of the minorities has been somewhat reversed in the <NAME> case.
Precedent	Of course, this was the first decision of this <ORG> which squarely dealt with the inter-relationship of Article 29(2) and Article 30(1).
Precedent	None of the earlier cited decisions did.
Precedent	326.
Precedent	The decision of this <ORG> in <CITATION_1806386> , 1951 SCR 525 cannot be construed as an authority for the proposition that Article 29(2) overrides the constitutional right guaranteed to the minorities under Article 30(1 ) , as Article 30(1) was not at all mentioned in the entire course of the judgment.
Precedent	Similarly, the <ORG> in <CITATION_1964375> , 1955 SCR 568 was not called upon to consider a situation of conflict between Article 30(1) and 29(2).
Precedent	<ORG>, was in fact directly concerned with Article 337 and an Anglo-Indian educational institution.
Precedent	In that background, when it was suggested that Article 29(2) was intended to benefit minorities only, the <ORG> negatived the submission as it would amount to a 'double protection', "double" because an Anglo-Indian citizen would then have not only the protection of Article 337 by way of a 60% reservation but also the benefit of Article 29(2).
Precedent	It was not held by the <ORG> that Article 29(2) would override Article 337.
Precedent	327.
Precedent	There is thus no question of striking a balance between Article 29(2) and 30(1) as if they were two competing rights.
Precedent	Where once the <ORG> has held:  "Equality of opportunity for unequals can only mean aggravation of inequality.
Precedent	Equality of opp ortunity admits discrimination with reason and prohibits discrimination without reason.
Precedent	Discrimination with reasons means rational classification for differential treatment having nexus to the constitutional permissible objects."
Precedent	and where Article 29(2) is nothing more than a principle of equality, and when "the whole object of conferring the right on minorities under Article 30 is to ensure that there will be equality between the majority and the minority, if the minorities do not have such special protection they will be denied equality", it must follow that Article 29(2) is subject to the constitutional classification of minorities under Article 30(1).
Precedent	328.
Precedent	Finally, there appears be an inherent contradiction in the statement of the <ORG> in St. Stephens that:  "the minority aided educational institutions are entitled to prefer their community candidates to maintain the minority character of the institutions subject of course to conformity with the University standard.
Precedent	The <ORG> may regulate the intake i n this category with due regard to the need of the community in the area which the institution is intended to serve.
Precedent	But in no case such intake shall exceed 50 per cent of the annual admission.
Precedent	The minority institutions shall make available at least 50 per cent of the annual admission to members of communities other than the minority community.
Precedent	The admission of other community candidates shall be done purely on the basis of merit."
Precedent	(p.614)  329.
Precedent	I agree with the view as expressed by the Learned Chief Justice that there is no question of fixing a percentage when the need may be variable.
Precedent	I would only add that in fixing a percentage, the <ORG> in St. Stephens in fact "reserved" 50% of available seats in a minority institution for the general category ostensible under Article 29(2).
Precedent	Article 29(2) pertains to the right of an individual and is not a class right.
Precedent	It would therefore apply when an individual is denied admission into any educational institution maintained by the <ORG> or receiving aid from the <ORG>  funds, solely on the basis of the ground of religion, race, caste, language or any of them.
Precedent	It does not operate to create a class interest or right in the sense that any educational institution has to set apart for non-minorities as a class and without reference to any individual applicant, a fixed percentage of available seats.
Precedent	Unless Articles 30(1) and 29(2) are allowed to operate in their separate fields then what started with the voluntary 'sprinkling' of outsiders, would become a major inundation and a large chunk of the right of an aided minority institution to operate for the benefit of the community it was set up to serve would be washed away.
Precedent	330.
Precedent	Apart from this difference with the view expressed by the majority view on the interpretation of Article 29(2) and Article 30(1).
Precedent	I am also unable to concur in the mode of determining the need of a minority community for admission to an educational institution set up by such community.
Precedent	Whether there has been a violation of Article 29(2) in refusing admissi on to a non minority student in a particular case must be resolved as it has been in the past by recourse to the <ORG>.
Precedent	It must be emphasised that the right under Article 29(2) is an individual one.
Precedent	If the non- minority student is otherwise eligible for admission, the decision on the issue of refusal would depend on whether the minority institution is able to establish that the refusal was only because it was satisfying the requirements of its own community under Article 30(1).
Precedent	I cannot therefore subscribe to the view expressed by the majority that the requirement of the minority community for admission to a minority educational institution should be left to the <ORG> or any other Governmental authority to determine.
Precedent	If the <ORG> is given the power to determine the requirements of the minority community in the matter of admission to its educational institutions, we would be subjecting the minority educational institution in question to an "intolerable encroachment" on the right under Article 30(1) and le t in by the back door as it were, what should be denied entry altogether.
Precedent	___________________________________________________________________________ S.N.
Precedent	<NAME>, J.
Precedent	1.
Precedent	We have had the advantage of going through the judgment of the learned Chief Justice of India, brother justice <NAME>, brother Justice <NAME> and sister Justice <NAME>.
Precedent	We are unable to agree with the views expressed by brother Justice <NAME> and sister Justice <NAME>.
Precedent	The learned Chief Justice has categorised the various questions into the following categories.
Precedent	1) Is there a fundamental right to set up educational institutions and, if so, under which provision;  2) Does the judgment in <NAME>'s case require reconsideration?
Precedent	3) In case of private unaided institutions can there be Government regulations and if so the what extent?
Precedent	4) In determining the existence of a religious or linguistic minority, in relation to Article 30 , what is to be the unit, the State or Country as a whole; and  5) To what extent the rights of aided mino rity institutions to administer be regulated.
Precedent	2.
Precedent	Justice <NAME> has dealt with categories 4 and 5 above.
Precedent	On other aspects he has agreed with the learned Chief Justice.
Precedent	3.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are in agreement with the reasoning and conclusion of the learned Chief Justice on categories 1 and 4.
RatioOfTheDecision	In respect of category 2 we agree with the learned Chief Justice that the cost incurred on educating a student in an unaided professional college was more than the total fee which is realized on the basis of the formula fixed in the scheme.
RatioOfTheDecision	This had resulted in revenue shortfalls.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out by the learned Chief Justice even though by a subsequence decision (to <NAME>) this <ORG> had permitted some percentage of seats within the payment seats to be allotted to Non-Resident Indians, against payment of a higher amount as determined by the authorities, sufficient funds were still not available for the development of those educational institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out by the learned Chief Justice experience has shown that most of th e "free seats" were occupied by students from affluent families, while students from less affluent families were required to pay much more to secure admission to "payment seats".
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out by the learned Chief Justice the reason for this was that students from affluent families had had better school education and the benefit of professional coaching facilities and were, therefore, able to secure higher merit positions in the common entrance test, and thereby secured the free seats.
RatioOfTheDecision	The education of these more affluent students was in a way being cross-subsidized by the financially poorer students who, because of their lower position in the merit list, could secure only "Payment seats".
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus we agree with the conclusion of the learned Chief Justice that the scheme cannot be considered to be a reasonable restriction and requires reconsideration and that the regulations must be minimum.
RatioOfTheDecision	However we cannot lose sight of the ground realities in our country.
RatioOfTheDecision	The majority of our population come from the poorer s ection of our society.
RatioOfTheDecision	They cannot and will not be able to afford the fees which will now be fixed pursuant to the judgment.
RatioOfTheDecision	There must therefore be an attempt, not just on the part of the <ORG> and the <ORG>, but also by the educational institutions to ensure that students from the poorer section of society get admission.
RatioOfTheDecision	One method would be by making available scholarships or free seats.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the educational institution is willing to provide free seats then the costs of such free seats could also be partly covered by the fees which are now to be fixed.
RatioOfTheDecision	There should be no harm in the rich subsidising the poor.
RatioOfTheDecision	4.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Chief Justice has repeatedly emphasised that capitation fees cannot be charged and that there must be no profiteering.
RatioOfTheDecision	We clarify that the concerned authorities will always be entitled to prevent by enactment or by regulations the charging of exorbitant fees or capitation fees.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are many such enactments already in force.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have not gone not the validity or otherwise of any such  enactment.
RatioOfTheDecision	No arguments regarding the validity of any such enactment have been submitted before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus those enactments will not be deemed to have been set aside by this judgment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of course now by virtue of this judgment the fee structure fixed under any regulation or enactment will have to be reworked so as to enable educational institutions not only to break even but also to generate some surplus for future development/expansion and to provide for free seats.
RatioOfTheDecision	5.
RatioOfTheDecision	We also wish to emphasis, what has already been stated by the learned Chief Justice, that an educational institution must grant admission on some identifiable and acceptable manner.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only in exceptional cases, that the management may refuse admission to a student.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, such refusal must not be whimsical or for extraneous reasons meaning thereby that the refusal must be based on some cogent and justifiable reasons.
RatioOfTheDecision	6.
RatioOfTheDecision	In respect of categories 3 and 5 we wish to point out that this <ORG> has been constantly taking the view that these aid ed educational institutions (whether majority or minority) should not have unfettered freedom in the matter of administration and management.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> which gives aid to educational institution including minority educational institution can impose such conditions as are necessary for the proper maintenance for the higher standards of education.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> is also under an obligation to protect the interests of the teaching and non-teaching staff.
RatioOfTheDecision	In many <ORG>s, there are various statutory provisions to regulate the functioning of these educational institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every educational institution should have basic amenities.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it is a school, it should have healthy surroundings for proper education; it should have a playground, a laboratory, a library and other requisite facilities that are necessary for a proper functioning of the school.
RatioOfTheDecision	The teachers who are working in the schools should be governed by proper service conditions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In <ORG>s where the entire pay and allowances for the teaching staff and non-teaching s taff are paid by the <ORG>, the <ORG> has got ample power to regulate the method of selection and appointment of teachers.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> can also prescribe qualifications for the teachers to be appointed in such schools.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly in an aided schools, <ORG> sometimes provides aid for some of the teachers only while denying the aid to other teachers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sometimes the <ORG> does not provide aid for the non-teaching staff.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> could, when granting aid, provides for the age and qualifications or recruitment of a teacher, the age of retirement and even for the manner in which an enquiry has to be held by the institution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words there could be regulations which ensure that service conditions for teachers and staff receiving aid of the <ORG> and the teachers or the staff for which no aid is being provided are the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	Pre-requisite to attract good teachers is to have good service conditions.
RatioOfTheDecision	To bring about an uniformity in the service conditions <ORG> should be put at liberty to prescribe the same without interv ening in the process of selection of the teachers or their removal, dismissal etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	We agree that there need not be either prior and subsequent approval from any functionaries of <ORG> (as the case may be) for disciplinary action, removal or dismissal.
RatioOfTheDecision	However principles of natural justice must be observed and as already provided, by the learned Chief Justice all such action can be scrutinised by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provisions contained in the various enactments are not specially challenged before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	The constitutional validity of the statutory provisions vis-a- vis the rights under Articles 19(1)(g), Article 26 , Article 29 and Article 30(1) of the Constitution can be examined only if a specific case is brought before the Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Educational Institution receiving <ORG> aid cannot claim to have complete autonomy in the matter of administration.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are found by various statutory provisions which are enacted to protect the interests of the education, students and teachers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Many of t he Statutes were enacted long back and stood the test of time.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nobody has ever challenged the provisions of these enactments.
RatioOfTheDecision	The regulations made by the <ORG>, to a great extent, depend on the extent of the aid given to institutions including minority institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In some <ORG>s, a lumpsum amount is paid as grant for maintenance of schools.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such cases, the <ORG> may not be within its right to impose various restrictions, specially regarding selection and appointment of teachers.
RatioOfTheDecision	But in some <ORG>s the entire salary of the teaching and non-teaching staff are paid, and these employees are given pension and other benefits, the <ORG> may then have a right and an obligation to see that the selection and appointment of teachers are properly made.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly the <ORG> could impose conditions to the effect that in the matter of appointments, preference shall be given to weaker sections of the community, specially physically handicapped or dependents of employees who died in harness.
RatioOfTheDecision	All such regulations may not b e said to be bad ad/or invalid and may not even amount to infringing the rights of the minority conferred under Article 30(1) of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Statutory provisions such as labour laws and welfare legislations etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	would be applicable to minority educational institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	As this decision is being rendered by a larger bench consisting of eleven judges, we feel that it is not advisable and we should not be taken to have laid down extensive guidelines in respect of myriads of legal questions that may arise for consideration.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our view in this case the battlelines were not drawn up in the correct perspective and many of the aggrieved or affected parties were not before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	7.
RatioOfTheDecision	As regards category 5, we agree with the conclusion of both the learned Chief Justice as well as Justice <NAME> that Article 29(2) applies to Article 30.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, we are unable to agree with the final reasoning that there must be a balancing between Articles 29(2) and 30(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	We, therefore, give our reasons for dis-agreeing with the fin al conclusion that there must be a balancing between Articles 29(2) and 30.
RatioOfTheDecision	8.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are conscious of the fact that the learned Chief Justice and Justice <NAME> have exhaustively dealt with the authorities.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, in our view there is need to emphasise the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are here called upon to interpret Articles 29(2) and 30.
RatioOfTheDecision	Submissions have been made that in interpreting these Articles the historical background must be kept in mind and that a contextual approach should be taken.
RatioOfTheDecision	We must, therefore, a) look at the history which led to incorporation of these Articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	The intention of the framers will then disclose how the contextual approach must be based; b) apply the well settled principles of interpretation; and c) keep the doctrine of "State Decisis" in mind.
RatioOfTheDecision	9.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the case of <CITATION_257876> , it has been held that in interpreting the provisions of a Statute or the Constitution it is the duty of the <ORG> to find out the legislative intent.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has been held that <ORG> debates are not conclus ive but that, in a Constitutional matter where the intent of the framers of the Constitution is to be ascertained, the <ORG> should look into the proceedings and the relevant data, including the speeches, which throw light on ascertaining the intent.
RatioOfTheDecision	In considering the nature and extent of rights conferred on minorities one must keep in mind the historical background and see how and for what purpose Article 30 was framed.
RatioOfTheDecision	10.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the case of <CITATION_1586918> reported in AIR (1984) SC 684 at page 686, it has been held as follows:  "Reports of the <ORG> which preceded the enactment of a legislation, reports of Joint <ORG> <ORG>, report of a <ORG> set up for collecting information leading to the enactment are permissible external aid to construction.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the basic purpose underlying construction of legislation is to ascertain the real intention of the <ORG>, why should the aids which <ORG> availed of such as report of a Special <ORG> preceding the enactment, existing state of Law , the environment necessitating enactment of legislation, and the object sought to be achieved, be denied to <ORG> whose function is primarily to give effect to the real intention of the <ORG> in enacting the legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such denial would deprive the <ORG> of a substantial and illuminating aid to construction.
RatioOfTheDecision	The modern approach has to a considerable extent eroded the exclusionary rule even in England."
Precedent	11.
Precedent	The partition of India caused great anguish, pain, bitterness and distrust amongst the various communities residing in India.
Precedent	Initially there was a demand for separate electorate and reservation of seats.
RatioOfTheDecision	However the principle of unity and equality for all prevailed.
RatioOfTheDecision	In return it was agreed that minorities would be given special protections.
RatioOfTheDecision	12.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason why Article 30(1) was embodied in the Constitution has been set out by Chief Justice <NAME> (as he then was) in the case of <CITATION_703393> .
RatioOfTheDecision	The relevant portion reads as follows: "The right to establish and administer educational institutions  of their choice has been conferred on religious and linguistic minorities so that the majority who can always having their rights by having proper legislation do not pass a legislation prohibiting minorities to establish and administer educational institutions of their choice.
RatioOfTheDecision	xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx  Every section of the public, the majority as well as minority has rights in respect of religion as contemplated in Articles 25 and 26 and rights in respect of language, script, culture as contemplated in Article 29.
RatioOfTheDecision	The whole object of conferring the right on minorities under Article 30 is to ensure that there will be equality between the majority and the minority.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the minorities do not have such special protection they will be denied equality.
RatioOfTheDecision	xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx xxx  The real reason embodied in Article 30(1) of the Constitution is the conscience of the nation that the minorities, religious as well as linguistic, are not prohibited from establishing and administering educational ins titutions of their choice for the purpose of giving their children the best general education to make them complete men and women of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	The minorities are given this protection under Article 30 in order to preserve and strengthen the integrity and unity of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	The sphere of general secular education is intended to develop the commonness of boys and girls of our country.
RatioOfTheDecision	this is in the true spirit of liberty, equality and fraternity through the medium of education.
RatioOfTheDecision	If religious or linguistic minorities are not given protection under Article 30 to establish and administer educational institutions of their choice, they will feel isolated and separate.
RatioOfTheDecision	General secular education will open doors of perception and act as the natural light of mind for our countrymen to live in the whole."
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis supplied) In the same Judgment, Justice <NAME> has held as follows: "Before we deal with the contentions advanced before us and the scope and ambit of Article 30 of the Constitution, it may be pertinent  to refer to the historical background.
RatioOfTheDecision	India is the second most populous country of the world.
RatioOfTheDecision	The people inhabiting this vast land profess different religions and speak different languages.
RatioOfTheDecision	Despite the diversity of religion and language, there runs through the fabric of the nation the golden thread of a basic innate unity.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a mosaic of different religions, languages and cultures.
RatioOfTheDecision	Each of them has made a mark on the Indian polity and India today represents a synthesis of them all.
RatioOfTheDecision	The closing years of the British rule were marked by communal riots and dissentions.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was also a feeling of distrust and the demand was made by a section of the Muslims for a separate homeland.
RatioOfTheDecision	This ultimately resulted in the partition of the country.
Precedent	Those who led the fight for independence in India always laid great stress on communal amity and accord.
Precedent	They wanted the establishment of a secular State wherein people belonging to the different religions should all have a feeling of equality and non-discrimination.
Precedent	Demand h ad also been made before the partition by sections of people belonging to the minorities for reservation of seats and separate electorates.
Precedent	In order to bring about integration and fusion of the different sections of the population, the framers of the Constitution did away with separate electorates and introduced the system of joint electorates, so that every candidate in an election should have to look for support of all sections of the citizens.
Precedent	Special safeguards were guaranteed for the minorities and they were made a part of the fundamental rights with a view to instil a sense of confidence and security in the minorities.
RatioOfTheDecision	Those provisions were a king of a Charter of rights for the minorities so that none might have the feeling that any section of the population consisted of first-class citizens and the others of second-class citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis supplied)  13.
RatioOfTheDecision	This was the basis on which minority rights were guaranteed.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rights were created so that minorities need have no apprehension that they would not  be able, either in the religious or in the educational fields, to do what the politically powerful majority could do.
RatioOfTheDecision	In matters of education what the politically powerful majority could do was to establish and administer educational institutions of their choice at their own expense.
RatioOfTheDecision	Principles of equality required that the minorities be given the same rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The protection/special right was to ensure that the minorities could also establish and administer educational institutions of their choice at their own expense.
RatioOfTheDecision	The demand for separatism and separate electorates was given up as principles of secularism and equality were considered more important.
RatioOfTheDecision	The principle of secularism and equality meant that <ORG> would not discriminate on grounds of religion, race, caste, language or any of them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus once <ORG> aid was given and/or taken then, whether majority or minority, all had to adhere to principles of equality and secularism.
RatioOfTheDecision	There never was any intention or desire to create a special or privileged class  of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	14.
RatioOfTheDecision	With this background, it is necessary to see how Articles 29 and 30 came to be framed/incorporated in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> was a strong advocate for minority rights.
Statute	Mr. <NAME> sent to <ORG> a Note with which he forwarded a draft Constitution.
Statute	This draft Constitution clearly indicates what rights were contemplated in framing, what is now, Article 30(1) Draft Article VI read as follows:  "The Right to Religious and Cultural Freedom (1) All citizens are equally entitled to freedom of conscience and to the right freely to profess and practise religion in a manner compatible with public order, morality or health :  Provided that the economic, financial or political activities associated with religious worship shall not be deemed to be included in the right to profess or practise religion.
Statute	(2) All citizens are entitled to cultural freedom, to the use of their mother tongue and the script thereof, and to adopt, study or use any other language and script of their choice.
Statute	(3) Citizens belonging to national minorities in a State whether based on religion or language have equal rights with other citizens in forming, controlling and administering at their own expense, charitable, religious and social institutions, schools and other educational establishments with the free use of their language and practice of their religion.
Statute	(emphasis supplied.
Statute	(4) No person may be compelled to pay taxes the proceeds of which are specifically appropriated in payment of religious requirements of any community of which he is not a member.
Statute	(5) Religious instruction shall not be compulsory for a member of a community which does not profess such religion.
Statute	(6) No person under the age of eighteen shall be free to change his religious persuasion without the permission of his parent or guardian.
Statute	(7) Conversion from one religion to another brought about by coercion, undue influence or the offering of material inducement is prohibited and is punishable by the law of the Union.
Statute	(8) It shall be the duty  of every unit to provide, in the public educational system in towns and districts in which a considerable proportion of citizens of other than the language of the unit are residents, adequate facilities for ensuring that in the primary schools the instruction shall be given to the children of such citizens through the medium of their own language.
Statute	Nothing in this clause shall be deemed to prevent the unit from making the teaching of the national language in the variant and script of the choice of the pupil obligatory in the schools.
RatioOfTheDecision	(9) No legislation providing <ORG>-aid for schools shall discriminate against schools under the management of minorities whether based on religion or language.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every monument of artistic or historic interest or place of natural interest throughout the <ORG> is guaranteed immunity from spoliation, destruction, removal, disposal or export except under a law of the <ORG>, and shall be preserved and maintained according to the law or the <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	This shows that the intention was to  give to the minorities the right to form, control and administer, amongst others educational institutions, at their own expense.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also to be noted that Article (9) is similar to what is now Article 30(2).
RatioOfTheDecision	As the educational institutions were to be at their own expense, <ORG> aid was not made compulsory.
RatioOfTheDecision	15.
RatioOfTheDecision	At this stage it must be remembered that the minorities to whom rights were being given, were not minorities who were socially and/or economically backward.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was no fear that economically, these religious or linguistic minorities, would not be able to establish and administer educational institution.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was also no fear that, in educational Institutions established for the benefit of all citizens, the children of these religious or linguistic minorities would not be able to compete.
RatioOfTheDecision	These rights were being conferred only to ensure that the majority, who due to their numbers would be politically powerful, did not prevent the minorities from establishing and administering their own educational  institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In so providing, the basic feature of the Constitution, namely, secularism and equality for all citizens, whether majority or minority was being kept in mind.
RatioOfTheDecision	16.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this behalf, an extract from <NAME>'s case is very relevant.
RatioOfTheDecision	It reads as follows:  "It may be recalled that as regards the minorities <ORG> had recognised in their report to <ORG> on May 6, 1946, only three main communities: general, Muslims and Sikhs.
RatioOfTheDecision	General community included all those who were non-Muslims or non-Sikhs.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> had recommended an Advisory Committee to be set up by <ORG> which was to frame the rights of citizens, minorities, tribals and excluded areas.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Cabinet <ORG> statement had actually provided for the cession of sovereignty to the Indian people subject only to two matters which were: (1) willingness to conclude a treaty with His Majesty's Government to cover matters arising out of transfer of power and (2) adequate provisions for the protection of  the minorities.
RatioOfTheDecision	Pursuant to the above and Paras 5 and 6 of the Objectives Resolution <ORG> set up <ORG> on January 24, 1947.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> was to consist of representatives of muslims, the depressed classes or the scheduled castes, the Sikhs, Christians, Parsis, Anglo-Indians, tribals and excluded areas besides the Hindus.
RatioOfTheDecision	As a historical fact it is safe to say that at a meeting held on May 11, 1949, a resolution for the abolition of all reservations for minorities other than the scheduled castes found whole- hearted support from an overwhelming majority of the members of the Advisory <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the schedule castes were concerned it was felt that their peculiar position would necessitate special reservation for them for a period of ten years.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would not be wrong to say that the separate representation of minorities which had been the feature of the previous <ORG> and which had witnesses so much of communal tension and strife was given up in favour of joi nt electorates in consideration of the guarantee of fundamental rights and minorities' rights which it was decided to incorporate into the new Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Objectives Resolution can be taken into account as a historical fact which moulded its nature and character.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since the language of the Preamble was taken from the resolution itself the declaration in the Preamble that India would be a Sovereign Democratic Republic which would secure to all its citizens justice, liberty and equality was implemented in Parts III and IV and other provisions of Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	These formed not only the essential features of the Constitution but also the fundamental conditions upon and the basis on which the various groups and interest adopted the Constitution as the Preamble hoped to create one unified integrated community.
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis supplied)"  17.
Fact	The draft Articles were then forwarded by <ORG> to <ORG>.
Fact	They were also forwarded to another <ORG> known as the <ORG> of M inorities.
Fact	These two Committees thereafter revised the draft and the revised draft was then forwarded to <ORG> for discussion.
Fact	The relevant portion of the revised draft read as follows:  "Rights relating to Religion  13.
Fact	All persons are equally entitled to freedom of conscience, and the right freely to profess, practise and propagate religion subject to public order, morality or health and to the other provisions of this Part.
Fact	Explanation 1.
Fact	- The wearing the carrying of kirpans shall be deemed to be included in the profession of the Sikh religion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Explanation 2.
RatioOfTheDecision	- The above rights shall not include any economic, financial, political or other secular activities that may be associated with religious practice.
RatioOfTheDecision	Explanation 3.
Precedent	- The freedom of religious practice guaranteed in this clause shall not debar the <ORG> from enacting laws for the purpose of social welfare and reform and for throwing open Hindu religious institutions of a public character to any class or section of Hindus.
Precedent	14.
Precedent	Every  religious denomination or a section thereof shall have the right to manage its own affairs in matters of religion and, subject to law, to own, acquire and administer property, movable and immovable, and to establish and maintain institutions for religious or charitable purposes.
Precedent	15.
Precedent	No person may be compelled to pay taxes, the proceeds of which are specifically appropriated to further or maintain any particular religion or denomination.
Precedent	16.
Precedent	No person attending any school maintained or receiving aid out of public funds shall be compelled to take part in the religious instruction that may be given in the school or to attend religious worship held in the school or in premises attached thereto.
Precedent	17.
Precedent	Conversion from one religion to another brought about by coercion or undue influence shall not be recognised by law.
Precedent	Cultural and Educational Rights  18.
Precedent	(1) Minorities in every unit shall be protected in respect of their language, script and culture, and no laws or regulations may be enacted that may operate oppr essively or prejudicially in this respect.
Precedent	(2) No minority whether based on religion, community or language shall be discriminated against in regard to the admission into <ORG> educational institutions, nor shall any religious instruction be compulsorily imposed on them.
Precedent	(3)(a).
RatioOfTheDecision	All minorities whether based on religion, community or language shall be free in any unit to establish and administer educational institutions of their choice.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) The <ORG> shall not, while providing <ORG> aid to schools, discriminate against schools under the management or minorities whether based on religion, community or language."
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus under Clause 18(3)(a) minorities based on religion, community and language were to be free to establish and administer educational institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constituent Assembly Debates, of 30th August, 1947, indicate that it was understood and clear that the right to establish and administer educational institutions was to be at their own expense.
Statute	During the Debate on 30th August, 1947, Mr. <NAME> d <NAME> proposed an amendment in Clause 18(2).
Statute	The suggested amendment read as follows:  "Provided that this clause does not apply to state Educational institutions maintained mainly for the benefit of any particular community or section of the people."
Statute	18.

RatioOfTheDecision	If we scan the various clauses of this article carefully and turn a sidelong glance at the next articles too, we will find that there are some inconsistencies or at least an inconsistency.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (1) of Article 22 imposes an absolute ban on religious instruction in institutions which are wholly maintained out of <ORG> funds.

Fact	The means, it would not be compulsory.

RatioOfTheDecision	There is no point about <ORG> aid, but I cannot certainly understand why the <ORG> should refuse recognition to those institutions established b y minorities where they insist on compulsory attendance at religious classes.

RatioOfTheDecision	Sir, to my mind, if you pursue such a course, the promises we have made to the minorities in our country, the promises we have made to the ear we shall have broken to the heart.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore I do not see any point why, in institutions that are maintained and conducted and administered by the minorities for pupils of their own community the <ORG> should refuse to grant recognition, in case religious instruction is compulsory.
RatioOfTheDecision	When once you have allowed them to establish schools according to their choice, it is inconsistent that you should refuse recognition to them on that ground.
RatioOfTheDecision	I hope something will be done to rectify this inconsistency."
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus it is to be seen that <NAME> is referring not just to draft Article 22 but also to draft Article 23(3)(a).
RatioOfTheDecision	He is pointing out that there is an apparent conflict  between these two Articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	Draft Articles 22 and 23(3)  (a) are, with minor changes, what are now Articles 28(3) and 30(1).
Precedent	Dr. <NAME> opposed the amendments proposed by <NAME> for various reasons, one of which is as follows:  "We have accepted the proposition which is embodied in Article 21 , that public funds raised by taxes shall not be utilised for the benefit of any particular community."

Precedent	24.
Precedent	On 8th December, 1948, when Pandit <NAME> moved his amendment, the debate read as follows:  "<NAME>: Sir, I beg to move.
Precedent	That for amendment No.
Precedent	687 of the List of amendments, the following be substituted:-  "That for Clause (2) of Article 23 , the following be substituted :- "(2) No citizen shall be denied admission into any ed ucational institution maintained by the <ORG> or receiving aid out of <ORG> funds of grounds only of religion, race, caste, language or any of them."

RatioOfTheDecision	Sir, I find there are three points of difference between this amendment and the provisions of the section which it seeks to amend.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first is to put in the words 'no citizen' for the words 'no majority'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly that not only the institutions which are maintained by the <ORG> will be included in it, but also such institutions as are receiving aid out of state funds.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thirdly, we have, instead of the words "religion, community or language", the words, "religion, race, caste, language or any of them".
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, Sir, it is happens that the words "no minority" seeks to differentiate the minority from the majority, whereas you would be pleased to see that in the Chapter the words of the heading are "cultural and educational rights", so that the minority rights as such should  not find any place under this section.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now if we read Clause (2) it would appear as if the minority had been given certain definite rights in this clause, whereas the national interests require that no majority also should be discriminated against in this matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	Unfortunately, there is in some matters a tendency that the minorities as such possess and are given certain special rights which are denied to the majority.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was the habit of our English masters that they wanted to create discriminations of this sort between the minority and the majority.
Fact	Sometimes the minority said that they were discriminated against and on other occasions the majority felt the same thing.
Fact	This amendment brings the majority and the minority on an equal status.
RatioOfTheDecision	In educational matters, I cannot understand, from the national point of view, how any discrimination can be justified in favour of a minority or a majority.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, what this amendment seeks to do is that the majority and the minority are brought on the same level.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ther e will be no discrimination between any member of the minority or majority in so far as admission to educational institutions are concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	So I should say that this is a charter of the liberties for the student-world of the minority and the majority communities equally.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second change which is amendment seeks to make is in regard to the institutions which will be governed by this provision of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Previously only the educational institutions maintained by the <ORG> were included.
RatioOfTheDecision	This amendment seeks to include such other institutions as are aided by <ORG> funds.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are a very large number of such institutions, and in future, by this amendment the rights of the minority have been broadened and the rights of the majority have been secured.
RatioOfTheDecision	So this is a very healthy amendment and it is a kind of nation-building amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, Sir, the word "community" is sought to be removed from this provision because "community" has no meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it is a fact that the existence of a community is determined by some  common characteristic and all communities are covered by the words religion or language, then "community" as such has no basis.
RatioOfTheDecision	So the word "community" is meaningless and the words substituted are "race or caste".
RatioOfTheDecision	So this provision is so broadened that on the score of caste, race, language, or religion no discrimination can be allowed.
RatioOfTheDecision	My submission is that considering the matter from all the standpoints, this amendment is one which should be accepted unanimously by this <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis supplied)  25.

Precedent	27.
Precedent	A reading of the <ORG> debates clearly show that the intention of the framers of the Constitution was that Article 29(2) was to apply to all educational institutions, including minority educational institutions under Article 30.
Precedent	28.
Precedent	This being the historical background and the intention of the framers, the contextual approach must also be one which gives effect to the minority rights but which does not elevate them into a special or privileged class of citizens.
Precedent	The contextual approach must therefore be that minorities have full rights to establish and administer educational institution at their own costs, but if they choose to take <ORG> aid they must then abide by the Constitutional mandate of Article 29(2) and with principles of equality and secularism.
Precedent	29.
Precedent	The same result follows if well settled principles of inte rpretation are applied.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is settled law that if the language of the provision, being considered, is plain and unambiguous the same must be given effect to, irrespective of the consequences that may result or arise.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also settled law that while interpreting provisions of a Statute, if two interpretations are possible, one which leads to no conflict between the various provisions and another which leads to a conflict between the various provisions, then the interpretation which leads to no conflict must always be accepted.
RatioOfTheDecision	As already been seen, the intention of the framers of the Constitution is very clear.
RatioOfTheDecision	The framers unambiguously and unanimously intended that rights given under Article 30(1) could be fully enjoyed so long as the educational institutions were established and administered at their own costs and expense.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once State aid was taken, then principles of equality and secularism, on which our Constitution is based, were to prevail and admission could not be denied to any student on grounds of r eligion, race, caste, language or any of them.
Precedent	30.
Precedent	A plain reading of <ORG> 29(2) shows that it applies to "any educational institution" maintained by the <ORG> or receiving aid out of <ORG> funds.
Precedent	The words "any educational institution" takes within its ambit an educational institution established under <ORG> 30(1).
Precedent	It is to be remembered that when <ORG> 29(2) [ i.e.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> 23(2)] was framed it was part of the same <ORG> which contained what is now <ORG> 30(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus it was clearly meant to apply to <ORG> 30(1) as well.
Statute	Significantly <ORG> 30 nowhere provides that the provisions of <ORG> 29(2) would not apply to it.
Statute	<ORG> 30(1) does not exclude the applicability of the provisions of <ORG> 29(2) to educational institutions established under it.
RatioOfTheDecision	A plain reading of the two <ORG>s indicates that the rights given under <ORG> 30(1) can be fully exercised so long as no aid is taken from the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is for this reason that <ORG> 30 does not make it compulsory for a minority educational i nstitution to take aid or for the <ORG> to give it.
Precedent	All that Article 30(2) provides is that the <ORG> in granting aid to educational institutions shall not discriminate against any educational institution on the ground that it is under the management of a minority.
Precedent	In cases where the <ORG> gives aid to educational institutions the <ORG> would be bound by the Constitutional mandate of Article 29(2) to ensure that no citizen is denied admission into the educational institution on grounds of religion, race, caste, language or any of them.
Precedent	By so insisting the <ORG> would not be discriminating against a minority educational institution.
Precedent	It would only be performing the obligation cast upon it by the Constitution of India.
Precedent	31.
Precedent	This interpretation is also supported by the wording of Article 30(2).
Precedent	Article 30(2) merely provides that the <ORG> shall not discriminate on the ground that it is under the management of a minority.
Precedent	To be noted that Article 30(2) does not provide that <ORG> shall not in granting aid impose  any condition which would restrict or abridge the rights guaranteed under Article 30(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	The framers were aware that when <ORG> aid was taken the principles of equality and secularism, which are the basis of our Constitution, would have to prevail.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clearly the framers of the Constitution considered the principle of equality and secularism to be more important than the rights under Article 30(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus in Article 30(2) it was advisedly not provided that rights under Article 30(1) could not be restricted or abridged whilst granting aid.
RatioOfTheDecision	A plain reading of Article 30(2) shows that the framers of the Constitution envisaged that certain rights would get restricted and/or abridged when a minority educational institute chose to receive aid.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must also be noted that when property rights were deleted [by deletion of Article 19(1)(f)] the framers of the Constitution realised that rights under Article 30(1) would get restricted or abridged unless specifically protected.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus Article 30(1A) was introduced.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 30(1A  ), unlike Article 30(2 ), specifically provides the acquisition of property of a minority educational institute must be in a manner which does not restrict or abrogate the rights under Article 30(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	When the framers so intended they have specifically so provided.
RatioOfTheDecision	Significantly even after Judgments of this Court (set out hereafter) which laid down that Article 29(2) applied to Article 30(1 ), the framers have not amended Article 30 to provide to the contrary.
Precedent	32.
Argument	Even though a plain reading of Articles 29(2) and Article 30 leads to no clash between the two Articles, it has been submitted by counsel on behalf of minorities that the right to establish and administer educational institutions be considered an absolute right and that by giving aid the <ORG> cannot impose conditions which would restrict or abrogate and/or abridge, in any manner, the right under Article 30(1).
Argument	It has been submitted that the right to administer educational institutions includes the right to admit students.
Argument	It has been submitted tha t the minorities, whether based on religion or language, have a right to admit students of their community.
Argument	It is submitted that this right is not taken away or abridged because <ORG> aid is taken.
Argument	It is submitted that notwithstanding the plain language of Articles 29(2) and 30 it must be held that the rights under Article 30(1) prevail over Article 29(2).
Precedent	33.
Precedent	To accept such an argument one would have to read into Article 30(2) words to the effect "state cannot in granting aid lay down conditions which would restrict, abridge or abrogate rights under Article 30(1)" or to read into Article 30(1) words to the effect "notwithstanding the provisions of Article 29(2)".
RatioOfTheDecision	Purposely no such words are used.
RatioOfTheDecision	A clash is sought to be created between Article 30(1) and 29(2) when no such clash exists.
RatioOfTheDecision	The interpretation sought to be given is on presumption that rights under Article 30(1) are absolute.
RatioOfTheDecision	As is set out in greater detail hereafter, every single authority of this <ORG>, for the past over 50 years, has held that  the rights under Article 30(1) are subject to restrictions.
RatioOfTheDecision	All counsel appearing for the minority educational institutions conceded that rights under Article 30(1) are subject to general secular laws of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	If rights under Article 30(1) are subject to other laws of the country it can hardly be argued that they are not subject to a constitutional provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	34.
RatioOfTheDecision	The interpretation sought to be placed not only creates a clash between Articles 29(2) and 30 but also between Article 30 and Article 15(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 15(1) prohibits the <ORG> from discriminating against citizens on grounds only of religion, race, caste, sex, place of birth or any of them.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the <ORG> were to give aid to a minority educational institution which only admits students of its community then it would be discriminating against other citizens who cannot get admission to such institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such an interpretation would also lead to clash between Article 30 and Article 28(3).
RatioOfTheDecision	There may be a religious minority educational institute se t up to teach their own religion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such an institute may, if it is unaided, only admit students who are willing to say their prayers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Yet once aid is taken such an institution cannot compel any student to take part in religious instructions unless the student or his parent consents.
RatioOfTheDecision	If Article 30(1) were to be read in a manner which permits <ORG> aided minority educational institutions to admit students as per their choice, then they could refuse to admit students who do not agree to take part in religious instructions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The prohibition prescribed in Article 28(2) could then be rendered superfluous and/or nugatory.
RatioOfTheDecision	Apart from rendering Article 28(2) nugatory such an interpretation would set up a very dangerous trend.
RatioOfTheDecision	All minority educational institutions would then refuse to admit students who do not agree to take part in religious instructions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In all fairness to all the counsels appearing for minority educational institutions, it must be stated that not a single counsel argued that Article 28(2) would not gov ern Article 30(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	All counsel fairly conceded that Article 30(1) would be governed by Article 28(2).
RatioOfTheDecision	One fails to understand how Article 30(1) can be held to be subject to Article 28(2) but not subject to Article 29(2).
Precedent	35.
Precedent	Accepting such an interpretation would also lead to an anomalous situation.
Precedent	As is being held all citizens have a fundamental right to establish and carry on an educational institution under Article 19(1)(g).
Precedent	An educational institution can also be established and maintained under Article 26(a).
Precedent	An educational institution could also be established under Article 29(1) for purposes of conserving a distinct language, script or culture.
Precedent	All such educational institutions would be governed by Article 29(2).
Precedent	Thus if a religious educational institution is established under Article 26(a) it would on receipt of <ORG> aid have to comply with Article 29(2).
Precedent	Similarly an educational institute established for conserving a distinct language, script or culture would, if it is receive <ORG> aid, have to c omply with Article 29(2).
Precedent	Such institution would also have been established for benefit of their own community or language or script or culture.
Precedent	If such educational institutions have to comply with Article 29(2) it would be anomalous to say that a religion or linguistic educational institution, merely because it is set up by a minority need not comply with Article 29(2).
Precedent	The anomaly would be greater because an educational institute set up under Article 26(a) would be for teaching religion and an educational institute set up under Article 29(1) would be for conserving a distinct language.
Precedent	On the other hand an educational institute set up under Article 30(1) may be to give general secular education.
Precedent	It would be anomalous to say that an educational institute set up to teach religion or to conserve a distinct language, script or culture has to comply with Article 29(2) but an educational institute set up to give general secular education does not have to comply with Article 29(2).
RatioOfTheDecision	It must again be remembered that  Article 30 was not framed to create a special or privileged class of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was framed only for purposes of ensuring that the politically powerful majority did not prevent the minority from having their educational institute.
RatioOfTheDecision	We cannot give to Article 30(1) a meaning which would result in making the minorities, whether religious or linguistic, a special or privileged class of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	We should give to Article 30(1) a meaning which would further the basic and overriding principles of our Constitution viz.
RatioOfTheDecision	equality and secularism.
Precedent	The interpretation must not be one which would create a further divide between citizen and citizen.
Precedent	36.
Precedent	It has also been submitted that a minority educational institute would have been established only for the purpose of giving education to students of that particular religious or linguistic community.
Precedent	It has been submitted that if Article 29(2) were to apply then the very basis of establishing such an educational institution would disappear once <ORG> aid is taken.
